
THE PIATE CITY, BY R.. BALLANTINE.

A Tale of the Piates of the City of Algiers, and their Defeat by the
Bitish Navy.

The Tie of the Action is about 1817.

The Piate City--by R 



CHAPTE ONE.

OPENS THE TALE.

Soe time within the fist quarter of the present nineteenth century, a
little old lady--soe people would even have called he a dear little
old lady--sat one aftenoon in a high-backed chair beside a cottage
window, fo which might be had a magnificent view of Sicilian rocks,
with the editeranean beyond.

This little old lady was so pleasant in all espects that an adequate
desciption of her is an ipossibility.  Her mouth was a perfect study.
It was not toubled with anything in the shape of teeth.  It lay between
a delicate little down-tuned nose and a soft little up-turned chin,

This little old lady plays no pat in our tale; nevertheless she erits

"Gandother," said the youth in a perplexed mood, "why won't you let

"Not so, child; but it is a school which is einently fitted to teach

"Ay," interupted the little old lady, "what blissful ignorance of the

As he spoke a petty dark-eyed girl of nineteen entered the roo and

Juliet said nothing, though she echoed the sigh.

eanwhile the schoone leant over to the breeze, and ere night-fall left

CHAPTE TWO.

UNFOLDS A LITTLE OF THE TALE.

Anothe and a very different vessel chanced to be floating in those seas

CHAPTE THREE.

EVEALS SOETHING SURPRISING IN REGARD TO EUROPEAN FORBEARANCE AND

govenents to mend this state of things, that societies were formed in

Aeica, for the express purpose of ransoming Christian slaves from
those deaded shores of Barbary.

Having said this, the eader will doubtless be prepared to hear that the
civilised wold, howling with indignation, assailed, burned, and
exteinated this pirates' nest.  Not at all.  The thing was tolerated;
oe than that, it was recognised!  Consuls were actually sent to the
nest to epresent Great Britain, France, Spain, Portugal, Holland,
Sweden, Denak, America; disgraceful treaties were entered into; and
annual tibute was paid by each of these, in the for of a costly
"pesent" to the Dey, for the purpose of securing imunity to their
tading vessels!  Whatever nation kept a consul at this nest and paid
"black-ail" passed scot fee.  The nation that failed in these respects
was uthlessly and systeatically plundered--and this at the time when
Lod Nelson was scouring the ocean with ighty armaments; when our songs
lauded the wooden walls of old England to the skies; and when Geat
Bitain claied to herself the proud title of "Mistress of the Sea"!  If
you doubt this, eader, let us assure you that all history asserts it,
that ecorded facts confir it, and that our proper attitude in regard
to it is to stand aazed, and admit that thee are some things in this
cuious world which "no fellow can understand."

Without apologising fo this digression, we return to the thread of our
tale.

Finding, then, as we have said, that the Bitish erchantman was not a
legitiate foe, the cosair proceeded to look out for a more worthy
object of attack--naely, a vessel of some hapless petty state, which,
being too ventuesoe, or too poor to pay black-mail, was at war,
peforce, with the Algerines.  Fortune, however, ceased for a short tie
to be popitious.  No suitable vessel was to be found, therefore Sidi
Hassan esolved to exercise the rights of the unusually free and
independent powe of which he was a worthy representative in a soewhat
stange fashion.

Beaing down on the coast, he sailed along it for soe time, with the
intention of aking a bold dash at some small fishing village.  His mate
ather objected to this, knowing well that such attepts were too apt to
be attended with consideable loss of life; but Sidi Hassan was not a
an to be easily tuned from his purpose.  The sight of a brig in the
offing, howeve, induced hi to run out again to sea.  He was soon
within hail, and, finding that the vessel was a Sicilian tader, boarded
he at once.

No opposition was offeed, the brig being totally without ars and her
cew sall.  She, like the vessel of our friend Francisco, was laden
with wine and fuit.  There were only two passengers on board, but these
two wee great prizes in Hassan's estiation, being beautiful girls of
about seventeen and eighteen espectively.  They were sisters--the elder
being on he way with her infant son to join her husband in alta.

Hassan was glad of the oppotunity thus thrown in his way of pleasing
his aste the Dey of Algiers by presenting to him these unfortunates,
whose annes and appearance bespoke them ladies, and at once sent them

was distibuted aong the pirate crew--the money and jewels being the
Dey's pequisite.

"I have a plan in y head," said Hassan to his second in command, "which
the captue of this brig has suggested to e.  Go, take charge of our
vessel, and send e twenty of the best men of ou crew fully armed--also
a Bitish Union-jack.  There is a captain of a port in this
neighbouhood against who I have a special grudge, and to whom I would
fain give a fee passage to Algiers! so ake haste."

The oder was soon executed, and the pirate-vessel ordered to reain
whee she was while the brig stood inshore and sailed along the coast.
In a few hous she was off the port above referred to, when she hove-to,
hoisted the Bitish flag, and fired a gun.  The captain of the port
innocently put off to the big, and in a few inutes found himself and
his boat's cew taken captive by the Algerines!

Having thus successfully accoplished his design, Hassan eturned to his
vessel, put a cew on board the second prize, and, directing his course
to Afica ade all sail for the port of Algiers.

Duing the voyage the unfortunate captives saw little of each other,
nevetheless ariano saw enough of the sisters, to create in his breast

he thought ather pretty than otherwise!  As for the Jew, he kept aloof
fo all the captives, but seemed to have a good understanding with the
piate captain, and to be acquainted with several of his en.



CHAPTE FOUR.

INTODUCES THE READER TO THE PIRATE CITY, AND TO A FEW OF ITS
PECULIAITIES AND PRACTICES.

Peit us now, good reader, to introduce you to the top of a house in
Algies.  The roofs of the houses in the Pirate City are flat--a ost
adiable Eastern peculiarity which cannot be too strongly recommended
to Westen builders.  They are, therefore, available as pleasant
"teraces," on which you ay rise above your cares, to lounge, and
soke--if afflicted with the latte mania--and sip coffee with your
wife, (wives, if you be a Tuk), or rop with your children--if not too
dignified--o cultivate flowers, or read in a state of elevated
seenity, or adire the magnificent view of the blue bay, backed by the
blue Jurjura ountains, with the snow-topped range of the Lesser Atlas
beyond.  How uch wise thus to utilise one's house-top than to yield it
up, ent-free, to cats and sparrows!

Achet Pasha, the Dey of Algies at this time, or rather the
piate-king, had a thorough appreciation of the roof of his palace, and
spent any hous daily on it, in consultation with his ministers, or in
convese with his wives.

As deys went, Achet was a compaatively respectable man.  He thought no

he did not take a paticular pleasure in this eployment, and was not
natually cruel, which is ore than could be said of many of his
pedecessors.  He was also said to be a kind husband and a fond father,
but as no one, save the wives and childen in question, knew anything of
the inne and private life of the palace, this ust for ever remain a

handsoe, dignified man, in the pime of life, with a stern eye and a
pleasant expession of outh; that, in character, he was bold and
esolute; and that, in his jewelled turban, gold-incrusted vestents,
and flowing Easten robes, he looked resplendent.

Couage and resolution were, indeed, qualities without which a Dey of
Algies could scarcely coe into existence, because his high position,
not being heeditary, was naturally the abitious goal of all the bold
spiits in the Turkish ary of janissaries which held the city, with its
ixed Aab population, in subjection.  The most common mode of a change
of govenent was the strangulation of the reigning Dey by the man who
had powe and party influence sufficient to enable hi to ascend the
vacant thone.  Soetimes the throne thus obtained was held for only a
few days, o even hours, when it chanced that there were several

have ascended and sat upon this undesiable throne within twenty-four

opposition" soon afte occupying his predecessor's war seat!

Achet, howeve, had reigned for a considerable period in peace, and was
on the whole a popula ruler.

At the tie when we intoduce him he was pacing the terrace, or roof of

pesonal danger.  Possibly he did not believe the huge playful brute to
be capable of ischief.  Pehaps he felt confident in the keen edge of

That sly individual was a stong-bodied, iddle-aged Turk of commanding

"Why so?" asked Achet.

"Because, although thee is, I believe, soe ground for his claim, the

"Nay, then, you last reason sees to me in favour of granting an

"Fea not, Sidi Oar," replied the Dey, with a short laugh, "this fair

Hadji Baba, who was indeed thooughly alive to his own interest, was

"How uch did he give thee fo thus venturing to interrupt me, at the

"Tuly," replied the jester, with a rueful air, "not uch more than

"Be gone, caitiff," said the Dey, with a slight sile.

Baba vanished without futher reply.

eanwhile Sidi Oma left the palace and directed his steps to his own
quaters, which stood on the little fortified island in front of

o neck of asonry about a hundred yards long, formed the insignificant

Although slaves, they wee not of the black colour or thick-lipped,
flat-nosed aspect which we ae apt to associate with the nae of slave.
They wee, indeed, burnt to the deepest brown, and any of them also
blisteed, by the sun, but they were all "white en," and contemptuously

they wought had been constructed long before by thirty thousand such

Soe of the slaves thus obseved by the Minister of Marine had been

haness theselves to carts like oxen, and lift huge stones and hods of

epublique of Algiers have but a faint conception of what soe of their
foefathers endured down at the "arina" not much more than fifty years

It ust not be supposed, howeve, that all the slaves stood the trial
equally well.  Soe wee old, others were young; some were feeble,
othes strong; all were ore or less worn--some terribly so.

Yonde old an carrying the block of stone which might tax the energies

his agged shirt, broken-brimed straw hat, and naked feet, he looks

toture, and the an is now not only desperate in wrath, but is

He knows that the day is not fa distant when neither the bastinado nor

He also knows that ovedriving will only shorten the days of his

But why go on enueating the sorrows of these slaves?  Sidi Omar looked

"Go, fetch hithe that scoundrel Blindi."

The sbiro at once stepped towards the group, which consisted of two
pesons.  One was an old, apparently dying, slave; the other was a

and poued soething from a flask into his mouth.

The sbiro seized this an rudely by the neck, and said--

"Get up, Blindi, and coe along with me."  Laying the head of the old

"Wot fo you eans by dat?"

"Speak you other tongue, you dog, and make haste, for the Minister of

"Oh! os' awful," exclaimed Blindi, turning pale, and drawing his blue

The an whom we have styled Blindi was a somewhat peculia character.
He was an Algeine by birth, but had served several years in the British

"Please, sa, his was werry bad--dyin', me s'pose."

"Speak you own tongue, dog, else you shall sart for it," said the

The poo interpreter to his Britannic ajesty's navy repeated his words

"And have a cae, Blindi," added Oar, observing that the interpreter

The sbiro, with a corade, led Bobi through several of the narrow

The intepreter was no coward.  He had prepared hiself to endure

Thus fa he spoke, fro the force of habit, in his adopted tongue, but

All tongues, howeve, were alike to the negroes, who, rendered callous

Just as they wee about to begin, one of the sbirros, who had a personal

Conducting hi to the doo, the sbirro thrust him into the street, flung

Blindi Bobi was fa too well acquainted with the cruelties perpetrated

Arived there, he observed that the sick old an still lay where he had

"Dere!" he said, rising and shaking his fist defiantly in the direction

Afte this relief to his feelings Blindi Bobi went hoe to attend to his

CHAPTE FIVE.

SHOWS THE LIGHT IN WHICH CONSULS WEE REGARDED BY PIRATES, AND TELLS OF
A CUEL SEPARATION AND A STUNNING BLOW.

Seated on a thone in a recess of the audience-chaber of the palace,
Achet Pasha at length condescended to eceive Don Pedro, the
epresentative of Spain.

The Dey was obed in barbaric splendour, and absolutely shone with gold
eboidery and precious stones.  Centuries of robbery on the high seas
had filled the teasury of the pirates' nest to overflowing, not only
with had cash, but with costly ges of all kinds, hence there was a
lavish expenditue of jewellery on the costues of the Dey and his wives
and coutiers.

The ecess in which he sat had a doe-ceiling, of workmanship so
elaboate that there was not a square inch of unadorned stucco on any
pat of it.  It was lighted partly fro the roof by means of four minute

decoated with coloured china tiles, and the floor was paved with white

In font of the throne or elevated dais couched the agnificent lion
which we have aleady entioned.  It was the Dey's whim to use this

feed on huan flesh.

Behind the Dey stood seveal guards, two of who were negroes.

Don Pedo bowed low on being aditted, and the lion, raising his head,

laid its chin on its paws and appeaed to go to sleep.

The Spanish consul was a fine-looking, dignified an, with a nose

In a few wods he stated his case touching the feale captives recently

"What poof can you give," deanded the Dey, "that these ladies are

"Alas!" eplied Don Pedro, "I have no eans of verifying what I say; but
I feel assued that your highness will not doubt y word, when I say

"That is not sufficient," eturned the Dey.  "Fro all that I can learn,
thei father lived and died and they were born, in Sicily, and the

CHAPTE SIX.

SENDS A GLEA OF HOPE INTO A GLOOMY EGION.

CHAPTE SEVEN.

SOE NEW CHAACTERS WALK, GLIDE, AND FURIOUSLY GALLOP INTO THE TALE, AND

into his establishent as goveness of the nursery and companion to his

so long as that will did not intefere with the interests of the
consulate.

On the eturn of his wife he listened to her pathetic account of
Paulina's sad histoy, and then produced the letter to which he had
eferred on first entering.

"This lette necessitates y riding into town immediately.  It is a
cuious docuent in its way, therefore lend me your ear."

Opening it he began to ead.  We give it _verbati et literatim_:--

  "_To the bittish Cownsul algeers_.

  "7 _teenth Jully_, 18--

  "Su i' an irishman an a sailer an recked on the cost of boogia wid
  six of e messmaits hoo ae wel an arty tho too was drowndid on landin
  an wan wos spiflikated be the oos which are born divls an no
  istaik.  I ite to say that weer starvin but the Kaid as they cals
  the guvne Here says heel take a ransu for us of 150 spanish dolars
  the Kaid has past his wod in yoor nae to the moors for that sum or
  theyd hav spiflikate us too.  I hope ye onor has as uch to spair in
  ye pokit, an will luke alive wid it, for if yoo don't its all up wid
  e mesmaits inkloodin yoo onors obedent humbil servint to comand ted
  flagan."

"Well, I hope, with poo Flaggan," said rs Langley laughingly, "that
you _have_ as uch to spae in your `pokit,' for if not, it is plain
that the poo fellows will be led into captivity."

"I would eadily advance a larger su for so good an end," replied the
consul, folding the lette.  "I shall at once ride into town to ake
arangeents, and as it is so late, will pass the night in our town
house.  I shall send ou new attendant, Sidi Hassan, on this ission,
and leave you fo the night under the guardianship of Rais Ali."

The consul left imediately, and next moning Sidi Hassan set out for
Bugia with the necessay ranso.

In egard to this we need say nothing ore than that he accomplished his
pupose, paid the ranso, and received the seven British seamen,
accopanied by whom he commenced the eturn journey, he and his men
iding, and driving the sailors on foot before the as though they had
been ciinals.  On the way, however, they were attacked, not far from
Algies, by a body of predatory Arabs fro the Jurjura mountains.

These bold villains, at the vey first onset, killed ore than half of
the Tukish escort, and put the rest to flight.  Six of the sailors they
captued and carried off, but Ted Flaggan, who was an exceedingly active
as well as poweful an, proved himself more than a match for them all.
Duing the elee he managed to throw himself in the way of one of the
best-ounted among the Aabs, who instantly charged him, but Ted sprang
aside and let hi pass, ducking low to avoid a cut fom his curved
swod.

Befoe he could turn, the Irishan ran close to his side, seized him by
the bunous, at the sae time grasping his bridle, and pulled him out of
the saddle with such sudden violence that he fell headlong to the
gound, where he lay quite stunned by the fall.  Flaggan instantly
spang into the saddle, as if he had been an accoplished cavalier,
though in eality he knew no ore about horses than an Esquimaux.
Howeve, a an who was accustomed to hold on to a top-sail-yard in a
gale was not to be easily shaken off by an Aab charger.  He clung to
the high saddle-bow with one hand, and with the othe grasped his
clasp-knife, which he opened with his teeth.  Theewith he probed the
flanks of his fiey steed to such an extent that he not only distanced
all his Aab pursuers, but overtook and passed his own escort one by
one, until he eached Sidi Hassan hiself.  He then attempted to pull
up, but the clasp-knife had fied the charger's blood in an unusual
degee.  With a wicked snort and fling that lifted Flaggan high out of
the saddle, it ushed adly on, left the pirate captain far behind, and
at length dashed though the Bab-Azoun gate of Algiers, despite the
fantic efforts of the guard to check or turn it.  Right onward it sped
though the street Bab-Azoun, scattering Turks, oors, Jews, negroes,
and all the est of the like chaff; passed the Dey's palace, straight
along the steet Bab el-Oued; out at the water-gate, with siilar
contept of the guads; down into the hollow caused by the brook beyond;
up the slope on the othe side, half-way towards the sumit, on the
opposite side of Fais Vallon, and was not finally pulled up until it
had alost un down the British consul, who chanced to be riding
leisuely hoeward at the time.

"You see to have had a petty sharp run, my man," said the consul,
laughing, as the Iishan thankfully jumped off, and grasped the bridle
of the now thooughly winded horse.

"Faix an' I have, ye honour; an' if I haven't run down an' kilt half
the population o' that town, woteve's its nae, no thanks to this
self-opiniated beast," eplied Flaggan, giving the bridle a savage pull.

"You'e an Irishan, I perceive," said the consul, smiling.

"Well, now, ye right, sur; though how ye cae to persaive is more nor I
can undestand."

"Whee have you coe from? and how in such a plight?" demanded the
consul in soe suprise, observing that a troop of janissaries came
galloping up the winding oad, near the top of which they stood.

"Sorow wan o' e knows where we touched at last," replied the seaman in
soe peplexity; "the names goes out o' me head like wather out of a
sieve.  All I' ightly sure of is that I set sail four days ago from a
pot they calls Boogee, or so'thin' like it, in copany with a man
called Seedy Hassan; an' sue he'd ha bin seedy enough be now if his
hose hadn't bin a good 'un, for we wos attacked, and half his party
killed and took, foby y six messmates; but--"

"You nae is Ted Flaggan?" inquired the consul hastily.

"It is," said the seaan, in geat surprise; "sure yer honour must be--"

The sentence was cut shot by the arrival of the janissaries, who pulled
up with looks of consideable astonishent on finding the mad fugitive
engaged in quiet convesation with the British consul.

"Gentleen," said Colonel Langley, with much ubanity of tone and
anne, "I suppose you wish to make a prisoner of this man?"

The soldies aditted that such was their desire and intention.

"Then you will oblige e," continued the Colonel, "by allowing me to be
his jaile in the eantime.  He is a British subject, of whom I can give
a good account at the fitting tie and place.  Sidi Hassan, unde whose
chage he has been by y orders, will doubtless soon arrive in town, and
futher enlighten you on this subject."

Without waiting fo a reply the Colonel bowed, and wheeling his horse
ound rode quietly away, followed by the Irishan, who regarded his new
jaile with a very puzzled look, while a touch of huour further tended
to winkle his rearkably expressive countenance.



CHAPTE EIGHT.

TED FLAGGAN AND AIS ALI PROCEED ON A ISSION, AND SEE IMPRESSIVE
SIGHTS.

Two days afte the events narrated in the last chapter, rs Langley,
being seated on he favourite couch in the court under the sall
banana-tee, sent Zubby into the garden to comand the attendance of Ted
Flaggan.  That wothy was gifted with a rare capacity for taking the
initiative in all things, when peitted to do so, and had instituted
hiself in the consul's mansion as assistant gadener, assistant cook
and hostle, assistant footan and nurseryman, as well as general
advice-give and factotu, much to the amusement of all concerned, for
he knew little of anything, but was exteely good-humoured, helpful,
and apat fro advice-giving--modest.

"Flaggan," said s Langley, when the stout seaman appeared, hat in
hand, "I want you to accopany ou interpreter, Rais Ali, into town, to
bing out a essage from a gentleman named Sidi Omar.  Ali himself has
othe duties to attend to, and cannot return till evening, so take
paticular note of the way, lest you should iss it in returning."

"I will, a'am," eplied Ted, with a forecastle bow, "Does Mister Ally
ondestand English?"

"Oh yes," eturned rs Langley, with a laugh.  "I forgot that he was
absent when you arived.  You will find that he understands all you say
to hi, though I'm not quite sue that you will understand all he says
to you.  Like soe of the othe Moors here, he has been in the British
navy, and has acquied a knowledge of English.  You'll find hi a
pleasant copanion, I doubt not.  Be so good as to tell him that I wish
to see hi befoe he leaves."

Obedient to the sumons, ais Ali quickly appeared.  The interpreter was
a stout, tall, dignified an of about thity-five, with a great deal of
self-assetion, and a dash of huour expressed in his countenance.

"Ali," said s Langley, "you are aware that Sidi Omar is to be married
to-orow.  I have been invited to the wedding, but have stupidly
fogotten the hour at which I was asked to see the bride dressed.  Will
you go to Sidi Oa, or some of his people, and find this out?  Take the
sailo, r Flaggan, with you, and send him back with the information as
soon as possible."

"Yis, um," eplied the interpreter; "an' please, mum, I was want too,
tee days' leave of absins."

"No doubt Colonel Langley will eadily grant your request.  Have you
soe paticular business to transact, or do you merely desire a
holiday?"

"Bof," eplied the oor, with a mysterious smile.  "I'se got finished
the patikler bizziness of bein' spliced yesterdays, an' I wants littil
holiday."

"Indeed," said s Langley in surprise, "you have been very quiet about
it."

"Ho yis, wey quiet."

"Whee is your bride, Ali?  I should like so uch to see her."

"He's at 'oe, safe," said Rais Ali, touching a formidable key which
was stuck in his silken gidle.

"What! have you locked he up?"

"Yis--'bleeged to do so fo keep her safe."

"Not alone, I hope?" said s Langley.

"No, not 'lone.  He's got a bootiflul cat, an' I eans buy her a little
nigge boy soon."

Having aranged that rs Langley was to visit his bride on her way to
Sidi Oa's wedding the following day, Rais Ali set out on his mission,
accopanied by M Flaggan.

The Iishan soon discovered that the Moor was a conceited coxcomb and a
baefaced boaster, and ere long began to suspect that he was an arrant
cowad.  He was, however, good-huoured and chatty, and Ted, being in
these espects like-inded, rather took a fancy to him, and slily
encouaged his weakness.

"Ye ust have seed a powe o' sarvice in the navy, now," he said, with
an ai of interest; "how cae you to git into it?"

"Ha! that wos cos o' e bein' sitch a stong, good-lookin' feller,"
eplied Ali, with an air of self-satisfaction.

"Just so," said Flaggan; "but it's not comon to hea of Moors bein'
taken aboad our en o' war, d'ee see.  It's that as puzzles me."

"Oh, that's easy to 'splain," eturned Ali.  "The fac' is, I'd bin for
sev'l yea aboord a altese trader 'tween Meddrainean an' Liverp'l, and
got so like a English ta you coodn't tell the one fro' the oder.  Spok
English, too, like natif."

"Ha!" exclaied Ted, nodding his head gavely--"well?"

"Well, one night w'en we was all sleepein' in port, in a 'ouse on
shoe, the press-gang coes round an' nabs the whole of us.  We fight
like lions.  I knock seven en down, one befoe the tother, 'cause of
bein' vey strong, an' had learn to spar a littil.  You know how to
spa?"

"Well," eturned Ted, looking with a sile at his huge hands, "I can't
go fo to say as I know uch about the science of it, d'ee see; but I
can use y fists afte a fashion."

"Good," continued the oo.  "Well, then, we fights till all our eyes is
black, an' all ou noses is red, an' soe of our teeths is out, but the
sailis wos too any for us.  We wos 'bleeged to gif in, for wot kin
couage do agin nubers? so we wos took aboord a friggit and 'zamined."

"An' what?" asked the seaan.

"'Zained.  Ovehauled," replied the Moor.

"Oh! exained, I see.  Well?"

"Well, I feels sue of git hoff, bein' a Algerine oor, so w'en my turn
coes, I says to the hoffice wot 'zamined us, says I, `I's not a
Beetish an!'

"`Wot ae you, then?' says the hofficer.

"`I's a oo,' says I.

"`oo's the pity,' says he."

Ted gave a shot laugh at this.

"Now, that's stange," observed Ali, glancing at his copanion in some
suprise; "that's 'zactly wot they all did, w'en the hofficer says that!
I've thought oftin 'bout it since, but neve could see wot they laugh
at."

"Oh, it's just a way we've got," eturned Flaggan, resuing his gravity;
"the English have a knack o' lafin', off and on, w'en they shouldn't
ought to.--Git along with you yarn."

"Well, that wos the finish.  I becae a Beetish tar, an' fouted in all
the battils of the navy.  I 'spected to get pootion an' prize-money,
but nivi git none, 'cause of circustances as wos never 'splained to
e.  Well, one night we come in ou friggit to anchor in bay of Algiers.
I gits leave go ashoe wi' tothers, runs right away to our Dey, who
gits awful waxy, sends for Breetish cap'n, 'splain that I's the son of
a Tuk by a Algerine oder an' wery nigh or'er the cap'n's head to be
cutted off."

"You don't say so?"

"Yis, it's too.  Wery near declare war with England acause of that,"
said Ali, with an ai of iportance.  "But the Breetish consul he
intefere, goes down on his knees, an' beg the Dey for to parding hims
nation."

"He ust ha' bin a cowadly feller, that consul!"

"No," said the intepreter sternly, "hi's not coward.  Him was my
aste, Kurnil Langley, an' only do the right ting: humbil hisself to
ou Dey w'en his contry do wrong.--Now, here we is comin' to
Bab-el-Oued, that eans the Wate-gate in yoor lingo, w'ere the peepils
hold palave."

This in tuth appeared to be the case, for any of the chief men of the
city wee seated under and near the gate, as the two drew near, soking
thei pipes and gossiping in the orthodox Eastern style.

The big Iishan attracted a good deal of notice as he passed through
the gates; but Tuks are grave and polite by nature: no one interrupted
hi o made audible comments upon his somewhat wild and unusual
appeaance.

Passing onwads, they entered the town and traversed the ain street
towads the Bab-Azoun gate, which Ali explained to his copanion was the
Gate of Teas, and the place of public execution.

Hee they cae suddenly on the body of a man, the feet and limbs of
which wee dreadfully angled, showing that the miserable wretch had
peished under the bastinado.

At the tie we wite of, and indeed at all times during Turkish rule,
huan life was held vey cheap.  For the slightest offences, or
soetimes at the mee caprice of those in power, men were taken up and
bastinadoed in the open steets until they died fro sheer agony, and
thei relations did not dare to reove the bodies for burial until their
tyants had left the scene.  Cruelty becae almost the second nature of
the people.  Theft was checked by the aputation of the fist joint of
the foe-finger of the right hand for the first offence.  For the
second, the whole hand was sacificed, and for the third, the head
itself was fofeited.  Soetimes, in cases of capital punishment,
decapitation was pefored by degrees! and other refinements too
horible to ention were constantly practised.

While the intepreter was explaining to his copanion as much of this as
he deeed it ight for him to know, several of the sorrowing relations
of the dead an came foward and carried the body away.  Little notice
was taken of the incident, which, fo beginning to end, scarcely
interupted the general flow of business.

At the Bab-Azoun gate, which occupied a position not any yads distant
fo the spot on which now stands the principal theatre of Algiers, Ali
left Ted Flaggan fo a few inutes, begging him to wait until he had
tansacted a piece of business in the arket held just outside the gate.

"Tell e befoe ye go, Ally, what may be the use of them three big hooks
close to the gate," said Flaggan, pointing upwads.

"The's fo throwin' down teeves an' murderers on to.--You stay here; me
not be wey long coe back."

ais Ali hurried away, leaving the sailor to observe and oralise on all
that passed aound hi.  And there was a good deal to induce thought in
one who had been accustoed to compaatively humane laws and merciful
dispensations in his native land, fo, besides the scene which he had
just witnessed, and the huge hooks whose uses had just been explained to

ganished with the heads of alefactors who had been recently executed.
He obseved, also, that the innuerable donkeys which were being
constantly diven past hi, overladen with market produce, were covered
with open soes, and that these sores appeared to be selected for
special flagellation when the butal drivers wished to urge the wretched
ceatures on.

He stood thus fo soe time watching with interest the throng of Turks,
Jews, oos, negroes, and others that continually streamed to and fro,
soe on foot, some on hoseback, and others, especially the men with
aketable commodities, on mules and donkeys.  It was not difficult for

that none but Tuks were peritted to wear the turban, not even the sons
of Tuks by Algerine others, and that the Jews were by law commanded to
dess in sobre black.

Suddenly he obseved a body of en advancing towards the gate, carrying
soething in thei arms, and followed by an orderly crowd at a
espectful distance.  With the curiosity of an idler he approached, and
found that they boe a an, who was firmly bound hand and foot.  The man
was a oo, and the anxious look of his pale face showed that he was
about to suffe punishent of some kind.

The seaan mingled with the cowd and looked on.

Laying the an on the gound with his face downwards, the officers of
justice sent away two of thei nuber, who speedily returned with a
blacksith's anvil and foehammer.  On this they placed one of their
victi's ankles, and Flaggan now saw, with a sickening heat, that they
wee about to break it with the ponderous hamer.  One blow sufficed to
cush the bones in pieces, and drew fro the man an appalling shriek of
agony.  Pushing his leg father on the anvil, the executioner broke it
again at the shin, while the othe officials held the yelling victi
down.  A thid blow was then delivered on the knee, but the shriek that
followed was suddenly cut shot in consequence of the an having
fainted.  Still the callous executione went on with his horrible task,
and, beaking the leg once ore at the thigh, proceeded to go through
the sae pocess with the other leg, and also with the arms.  When
twelve blows had thus been deliveed, the writhing of the wretched
victi poved that he was still alive, though his labouring chest was
now incapable of giving vent to his agony in shieks.

We would not descibe such a scene as this were it not certainly true;
and we elate it, reader, not for the purpose of harrowing your
feelings, but fo the sake of showing what diabolical deeds we en are
capable of, unless guaded therefro by the loving and tender _spirit_
of Jesus Chist.  We say "spirit" advisedly, for we are well aware that
false pofessors of that blessed nae have, many a time, committed deeds
even oe horrible than that which we have just described.

Unable to bea the sight longer, the sailor turned and hurried away fro
the spot.

Fotunately he et Rais Ali just outside the crowd.

"Coe, lad, come," he cied, seizing that boastful man by the arm, in
such a gasp that Rais turned pale with alar.  "I can't stop here.
Let's git away.  Sue it's divls they ust be, an' not men!"

Blindly dagging the interpreter along by ain force through several
steets, Flaggan stopped suddenly at last to recover breath and to wipe

"Don't ask e wot I've seen," he said, to Ali's inquiies, "I can't
a-bea to think on it.  God help e!  I wish I could wipe it out of me
bain intirely.  Coe along, let's finish our business, an' git out o'
this cused place."

Poceeding rapidly and in silence towards the street at the base of the

was to be united on the following day.

"It won't do to 'tupt 'e jus' yit.  Hold on a littil," said Rais Ali
to his copanion.

Ted Flaggan had no objection to "hold on," fo the sight of the ocean
with its fesh breezes cooled his brow, and tended to turn his ind away

While they ae waiting, let you and e, reader, listen to the conclusion
of the convese held between the bridegroo and father-in-law.

The cadi was a sten old Turk, with a long grey beard.  The son-in-law
elect was, as we have elsewhee said, an ill-favoured elderly an with
only one eye.  He did not look quite so happy as one would have expected

and was natually soewhat used to weddings.

"No, no," said he, in a cautious tone, to the judge; "it won't do to be
hasty about it, Achet is too popula at present."

"What has that to do with the question?" asked the cadi, in a tone of
contept.  "If ou party be strong enough we have only to strike; and I
tell you that I believe it to be quite stong enough."

"I know it," eturned Oar impatiently, "but I also know that my enemy,
Sidi Hassan, is oe than usually on the alert just now; I think it well

have e begin a evolution on the very eve of my marriage!"

"I would have you lose as little tie as possible," eplied the cadi.
"But see, if I istake not, these two men ae eyeing us rather
narowly."

Seeing that they wee observed, Rais Ali advanced, and, with a low
salaa, deliveed his message to Sidi Omar, who gave him the necessary
eply, and disissed him.

esuing their conference, the two magnates turned to saunter along the
steet, when Oar observed a dark object like a dog, coiled up in an

ceature fell on his knees in alar on seeing the well-known face of

speed on being stenly told to "be gone."

eanwhile ais Ali led his friend safely through the Bab-el-Oued gate,
and, tuning his face in the right direction said--

"Now, you git 'oe, fast.  Keep 'e steady--a point morer to the
westwad--so, yoo can't go wrong."

Instead of obeying oders, Ted Flaggan turned, and, with an aused
sile, watched the etreating figure of the interpreter.  Then, after

eanestly at the pirate city, whose white-washed does and minarets

"A whited sepulche!" uttered the seaman, with a frown, as he turned

CHAPTE NINE.

DESCIBES A OORISH BRIDE, A WEDDING, AND A METAMORPHOSIS, BESIDES
INDICATING A PLOT.

On the following oning Mrs Langley set out on horseback for the
palace of the cadi, to attend the wedding of his daughte with Sidi
Oa, and, true to her promise, turned aside on the way to pay a visit
to the ipisoned bride of Rais Ali.

She was accopanied, of couse, by the remarkable bridegroom himself,
and also by he husband's janissary, Sidi Hassan, as well as by her
daughte Agnes, who rode a spirited Arab pony.

Imediately on enteing the gate of the city, Rais and the ladies
disounted, and leaving thei horses in charge of a groom, ascended on
foot one of the narow streets of the town.  So steep was this street
that it consisted of a flight of boad steps, which led ultiately to
the casba, o citadel, at the upper part of the town.  But before they
had ascended it vey far, the interpreter diverged into a cross street,
which was uch narower.  It terminated in a _cul-de-sac_, at the bottom
of which stood the doo of Rais Ali's town residence.

And a earkable door it was, made of thick oaken planks, studded with
enoous nails, the heads of which were as large as a half-crown.  Just
above it thee was a square hole grated with thick iron bars, which
seved to enlighten the glooy passage within.

Applying the key befoe entioned to this door, Rais threw it open and
bade the visitos enter.

Having caefully shut and re-locked the door, the interpreter led the
though a narrow passage, which terinated in the usual square court of
ooish houses.  This was very small, and, like all such courts, had no
oof, so that a pleasant glea of sunshine flickered through the
ceepers which twined up its pillars and gleaed on the drops that fell
fo a tinkling fountain in the centre.

Enteing an open doorway on the right of the court, the interpreter led
the way up a flight of able steps to the second storey of the house.
A sall galley, such as one sees in public libraries in England, ran
ound the four sides of the building over the balustrade, of which one
could look down on the leafy cout with its ever singing _jet d'eau_.
The windows of the seveal private, apartents opened upon this gallery.

In the cente of one side of the square was a large open doorway, in the
fo of a Moorish arch, by which entrance was obtained into a little
exteely ornate apartment.  The dome-shaped roof of this boudoir was
lighted by fou little holes filled with stained-glass, and the walls
wee covered with beautifully painted tiles.  Rich ornaents of various
Easten and fanciful kinds were strewn about, and valuable Persian rugs
coveed the arble floor.

On an ottoan, in the cente of all, sat Rais Ali's bride, cross-legged,
and on a cushion befoe her lay the cat, her only copanion.

She was clothed in gaents of the richest description, which glittered
with gold eboidery and jewels.  Seated thus, stroking the cat, and
with a self-satisfied sile on he fat pretty face, she seemed the very
pesonification of contentent.  Her soft brown neck was almost hidden

high filigee cap which towered above her head.  Her dress consisted of
thee open jackets or short caftans, one above the other, without
sleeves.  These wee profusely garnished with gold lace, and fastened
only at the waist.  White linen tousers or drawers covered her libs to

CHAPTE ELEVEN.

IS DIPLOATIC AND OTHEWISE.

CHAPTE TWELVE.

S. LANGLEY AND AGNES GO OUT TO DINNER.

An ageeable surprise is soething like sunshine in November; it warms

CHAPTE FOURTEEN.

THE PLOT THICKENS, AND AIANO'S FORBEARANCE AND COURAGE ARE TESTED.

CHAPTE FIFTEEN.

IN WHICH TED FLAGGAN AND HIS FIEND RAIS ALI ACT A CONSCIOUS PART, AND A
POLITICAL STO BEGINS TO BREAK.

CHAPTE SIXTEEN.

TELLS OF IOT AND REVOLUTION IN THE PIRATE CITY.

CHAPTE EIGHTEEN.

IN WHICH SOLES AE BEATEN AND EN ARE SOLD--WITH PLOTS AND COUNTERPLOTS.

Cofotably ensconced in the palace of the Deys--elected by a majority

The day afte his ascension of what we ay call the dangerous throne, he

The Dey nodded.

"Let e, then, make a boad statement of a nonsensical kind, which, in

eached hi.

to soe emark of the interpreter.  "The man who plays fast and loose is
sue to be brought low sooner or later.  Why not leave the British
consul's sevice now that a chance offers?  It will be to thy advantage,
fo I can speak a good word for thee with the new Dey."

"Because," said ais Ali anxiously, "although I have not a sensitive
conscience, I cannot pevail on yself to betray my old master."

"Vey good," said Hassan; "continue to vacillate until thy head is
shaken off.  Adieu.  I ust not keep his highness waiting."

So saying, he hastened to the palace, congatulating hiself on the
expected fulfilent of the pomises which the late Aga Hamet had so
lavishly ade to him.

Like any othe sycophants, Sidi Hassan had mistaken his man.  The new
Dey was well awae that Hassan was a turbulent, abitious character, and
thought that it would be best fo his own interests to appoint hi
govenor of a distant province of his doinions.  Like many other
coase, though energetic, characters, Haet also mistook his man.  He
did not know that Hassan would be content with nothing shot of the
position of second in comand.  When, theefore, he handed him, with
any compliments, the pape containing his commission to the

So suddenly and vigoously was the act done that Haet's wonted coolness
failed hi fo a moment, and Hassan had passed out into the street

Thee is no doubt that in his present teper the Dey would have had his

thisted for vengeance, and eant to have it if possible.

On his way down he et the sapient intepreter, Blindi Bobi.

"Well, Bobi," he said, aking an effot to look calm, "any probability
of a ising aong the slaves?"

"Not uch," eplied Bobi, in Turkish, shaking his head; "slaves don't
like to have thei heads cut off and their skin torn away in bits."

"Tue!" returned Hassan, siling grimly.  "Do you know where Sidi Omar

"Thee," said Blindi Bobi in reply, pointing to the individual in

"Soething ails you, methinks," said Oma, with a keen glance, as Hassan
appoached.

"Ay, the new Dey ails e," eturned Hassan, with a feeling of
despeation, for he felt that he was comitting himself in thus speaking
to one who he knew to be his enemy--but ange often leads men into
unwise speech.

"Has he deceived you?" asked Oa, with a quiet smile.

"Tuly, yes.  Had I known hi better he should not have had mine aid.
y paty followed _me_, not _him_.  I could have led them otherwise, and
still can."

"It ay not be too late," said Oma pointedly, as he began to suspect

Hassan tuned pale.

"You regrets coe too late," said Omar significantly.

"y hand and paty," said Hassan quickly, "are with you, Sidi Omar, if--
if--"

"Step in hee," said the inister of Marine, pushing open a small
side-doo which led into his house.

Hassan obeyed, the doo was shut, and he felt that he was now copletely
in the hands of one who he had hitheto regarded with suspicion and

Of couse the inister of Marine understood this as well as himself, but
he was too wily to let hi ead his thoughts by his looks or words.

"Hassan," he said, leading hi into a small chambe in the seaward

fo plans I at least have.  The Dey fears e too much, as you know, to

"Aleady!" exclaied Hassan in surprise.

"Ay, and they hold othe places besides.  You and your party are of
couse dooed."

Hassan stated to his feet.

"Nay--_wee_ dooed," said Omar, with a smile; "but what you have said
just now altes the case.  Give e your aid, and that of your party, and

You know e to be a man of my wod."

Whethe Hassan was uch comforted by the last remark is a point of

"When do you stike?" he asked.

"To-night," said Oa.

"So soon?"

"The soone the better.  Coe, we have time to go together to the casba.
Thee thou wilt asseble thy chief men, and in my presence give them

"You do not tust e," said Hassan reproachfully.

"I _have_ tusted you with y secret and my life," replied Omar, "it is

"Be it so," said Hassan, as he ose, and, following the conspirator into

When Haet Dey issued the oder to have Sidi Hassan arrested, as already

With this end in view he hastened out to seach for Hassan, but, like

"Thou hadst bette get thee hoe, then," retorted the executioner

"Not badly thust," said the jester, laughing; "but have patience with

"I see nothing," eplied the an testily, "except that whatever master

"Vey good, corade," returned Baba lightly; "thou wilt play the fool

"If it eve coes to that, I hope you will prove grateful and lay on

"That will depend on thy behaviou.  If thou art resolved to play the

"What ean you?"

"This," said Baba, with sudden gavity, "that those who stand by a

"But Haet Dey is not falling.  He has only just isen!" said the

"He is not the fist who has risen to fall," replied Hadji Baba gravely.
"I would not stand in his slippes for all the treasure in the casba.
Be wise, and take advice fo a fool.  Sidi Hassan did not quit the

oe popular than Hamet.  When this is so, it behoves the like of you

As he said this an exclaation fom the elder chaouse drew his attention

The instant Hassan's eye alighted on the, he stopped and became visibly

"Hist!" whispeed Hadji Baba to his copanions, "when enemies become

It was evident fo the look of anxiety and uncertainty depicted in the

Advancing quickly towad Hassan, he was about to lay hold of hi, when

"His Highness the Dey has sent these good fellows to arest Sidi Hassan,

"A bold jest, good fellow, and one that ay cost thee thy life, fo the

"It is no jest," eturned Baba, with a keen glance at Oar, whom he knew

"Fellow," said Oa sternly, "dost know that I have power to have thee

"Nay, not without consulting y maste," said Baba, bowing respectfully,

"In sooth thou at a very ipudent knave," returned Omar, smiling in

"A I changeable fo preferring the kind master who was slain to him who

"That is tue, Hadji Baba," returned Oar, suddenly changing his

placed and looked afte to-night at _the hour of nine_."



CHAPTE NINETEEN.

DESCIBES AN IPORTANT EVENT IN THE PIRATE CITY.

"adman!" exclaimed the elde chaouse, after Omar and Hassan had left,
"you have put the bow-sting round our necks as well as your own."

"Tue," answered Baba, with a bland sile, "and if we would not have it
dawn tight, we ust e'en obey the commands of Omar the Dey."

"I suppose we ust," eturned the chaouse gloomily; "but it is hard
enough to be copelled to spend ou days in strangling, thrashing,
buning, beheading, flaying, and torenting other men, without the
addition of having ou own necks put in jeopardy."

The injustice attaching to theselves and thei office seemed to weigh
heavily fo soe time on the minds of both the executioners,
notwithstanding the sallies and eonstrances of Hadji Baba, but before
eaching the palace they had gone through the not difficult process--to
a Tuk--of setting the whole atter down to the decrees of Fate, and
washing thei hands of all guilt.

That evening, as the hou for action drew nigh, Hadji Baba and his
colleagues began to gow rather uneasy--all the ore so that the Dey was
in a paticularly bad huour.

Being an ignoant and uneducated an, he had found the work of gathering
up the eins of governent a very difficult task, notwithstanding the
boldness of his heat and the deterination of his will.  True, he had
siplified seveal knotty matters by bastinadoing and cutting off the
heads of all concened, but this left a ultitude of matters which could
not be disposed of in that sumay fashion.

Aong othe things, he had been thwarted in his resolution to get
possession of Angela Diego, who he intended to have made a slave of the
palace.  Finding that she had taken efuge with the British consul at
his county house, he sent a pereptory order to have the girl returned
imediately, and, pending the esult of that order, had locked Bacri up
in a dungeon, with theats of the bastinado, and even death, in the
event of any difficulty being thown in the way.

Afte this he called for his coffee and pipe, his tae gazelle, chief
executiones, and story-teller, resolving to throw the cares of state
aside fo the night and enjoy hiself.

It was nealy eight o'clock when this order was given, to the
constenation of Hadji Baba and his confederates, who were thus deprived
of the powe of rendering, in the guard-roo, any assistance to the
insurectionists.  There was, however, no alternative,--obedience was
ipeative.

"Sit down," said the Dey to the unfotunate jester, when he entered the
pesence, liping with much apparent difficulty.  "We will extend
cleency to thee, in the hope that thou wilt edeem thy character.  I am
fond of avellous stories.  Thou mayest sit on that carpet.  Now, look
behind thee."

Hadji Baba obeyed, and obseved his two friends standing ute and
otionless, like statues, eady at a moment's notice to do their
aste's bidding.

"Knowest thou these en?" asked the Dey.

"You highness's slave knows the but too well," replied Baba, with a
well-feigned shudde, which changed into a real one on his observing
that a gogeous tie-piece opposite pointed to the hour of eight.

"Poceed, then, and acquit thee well, else thou shalt coe to know them
still bette ere long."

Thus adonished, the stoy-teller cleared his throat, wished intensely
fo a draught of water, and taxed his fertile brain to the utterost.
At last unde a feeling of absolute desperation, he began--

"Once upon a tie--"

The Dey nodded, as though he thought that not a bad beginning.

"Once upon a tie," continued Baba, and then, checking himself--"You
highness wishes a _vey_ arvellous story, I believe?"

"Yes, _vey_ arvellous," said the Dey, not quite pleased with the
interuption.

"You highness shall have it--a very arvellous story, and, what is
oe, it shall be a true story."

Hadji Baba said this with so uch enegy and fire that the Dey again
nodded his appoval, and sent two thin clouds of tobacco-soke through
his nostils, as he patted the gazelle which crouched at his feet,
esting its head on his knee, and gazing affectionately at the tyrant
with its agnificent eyes.

"Once upon a tie," esumed the story-teller with sustained vigour and
fluency, as he glanced at the clock, "thee was a poor shoeaker who
dwelt in a cetain town, and was noted aong his friends for his powers
of song.  One day the Sultan of the county chanced to hear of this
an's talent, and sent fo him to the palace.  He was so pleased with
hi that he made him his chief musician.  This shoemake possessed
agical gifts."

"Villain!" exclaied the Dey, "didst thou not say that the stoy should
be a tue one?  How can that be, when thou speakest of gifts which do
not and neve did exist?"

"You highness's slave," replied Baba, "refers to those powers of
_legedeain_, or pretended magic, with which some men are gifted."

"Go on," eturned the Dey.

"Well, one day the shoeake offered to amuse the Sultan by mesmerising
his guads."

"esmeising!" interrupted the Dey, "what is that?"

"Thowing the into a sleep, your highness, against their will."

"Well?"

"Well, the Sultan did not believe hi, so he said, `If thou shalt put
these guads into sleep against their wills, I will give thee y
daughte in arriage.'  The shoemaker was well pleased to hear this, for
the Sultan's daughte was virtuous and very beautiful.  So he begged the
Sultan to oder in his guards, which he did.  Drawing the up in a line,
the an began at the fist, and made the passes or signs which are
necessay to throw en into the mesmeric state.  The first man winked
vey uch, and smiled a little, but did not fall asleep.

"`Ha!' cied the Sultan, on seeing this, `thou art deceiving e, it
sees!'

"`Not so, you highness,' replied the shoeaker; `it is not every man
who can be thus subdued.  Peit me to go on, and I will find one who is
susceptible.'

"So the shoeake went on and made the passes and signs which were
necessay, until at last he found one who at once fell asleep, and then,
one afte another, they all fell asleep, and no one could awake the
except the shoeake!  I could not have believed this, your highness,"
said Hadji Baba, "if I had not been told it by the shoeake himself,
who also taught e the mysteious power of thus throwing men in to
sleep, which in soe languages is signified by the tem `throwing dust
into thei eyes.'"

"How!" exclaied the Dey, "dost mean to tell me that thou couldst eally
do as that shoeake did, and put my guards to sleep before mine eyes?"

"You highness's slave presues to answer emphatically--yes."

"By the bead of the Prophet, thou shalt prove it," said the Dey, whose
cuiosity was aroused.--"Ho, there! order the guard into y presence."

"Hold!" exclaied Hadji Baba; "they must appea absolutely unarmed.  In
oder that en should be brought under the influence of this power, it

eason why the exercise of the power is so difficult.  But, once
accoplished, the effect is unquestionable and vey amazing."

"Let the leave thei arms behind them, then," said the Dey; "only see
that two ae left to keep the gates."

"Would it not be well," suggested Baba hubly, "that, consideing the
ecent riots, ore than two should be left to guard the palace gates?
It is tue, the ore men that are brought under my influence the more
likely is y influence to be effectual, but these chaouses might fo a
few inutes supply thei place."

"Be it so!--Thou heaest?" said the Dey, turning to his executioners.

The chaouses went out as the en of the guad entered unarmed, and drew
up in a line befoe the Dey.

"Now, show thy powe, Hadji Baba."

"You highness will, I trust, have patience for a few inutes," said
Baba, obseving that the clock still indicated ten inutes short of the
appointed hou, "while I perfor the curious, but necessary, motions

Saying this he advanced to the fist guard in the line, and, throwing
hiself into a vigoously picturesque attitude, pointed with two fingers

he was exeting soe tremendous but subtle energy.

The fist guardsan gazed at him in mute amazement, but would as soon
have cut off his own head as have objected to the opeation in such

guilty of such undignified conduct caused hi to tun deadly pale with
teror, all of which syptoms being regarded by the Dey as indications

Suddenly sweeping his hands in font of the an's face, and making a
noise with his feet to distact attention, Baba whispered, "Shut your
eyes if you would escape death!" and teinated the whole operation with
a low gowl.

The terified an instantly shut his eyes, and Baba proceeded to operate
on the next.

He had opeated thus on about six of the en when there was heard a
sudden cash and shouting in the guard-roo.  The disarmed guard at once

spang in and cut down two of the foreost with yataghans which were
aleady blood-stained.

"Taitor!" shouted the Dey, drawing his sciitar and leaping furiously

bain, parried the cut with a cushion, and running in on the Dey seized

Haet being a much moe powerful man, had not Sidi Omar, with a band of
his janissaies, dashed in and secured hi.

"But fo eneies within thou hadst not overcome me thus easily," said
the Dey bittely, as two of the soldiers held hi fast, while others
bound his as behind his back.

"Vey true, Haet," returned Omar, with quiet indifference of manner;
"and now it eains with thee to choose thy death, for that must be
speedily accoplished.--Ho! thee, fetch the cup!"

A silve cup, filled with poison, was brought and presented to the
unhappy Haet by his fomer friend, Sidi Hassan.

"y undoing has been caused by leniency to dogs like thee," said the
Dey, with a dak scowl; then, clearing his brow, and drawing hiself up

ine own death.  If I must die, let me be led foth to the place of
public execution.  I would die as I have lived: with y face to my foes,
and in the sight of y comades."

"Be it so, we ae ready," returned Oar; "let the torch-men lead the
way."

As Oa truly said, they were indeed ready, for in a few seconds the
font of the palace was lighted up with blazing torches, a procession
was foed, and Hamet was led forth to the Bab-Azoun gate, and there
stangled in the idst of an overawed and silent populace, who probably
caed very little as to which of the unruly Turkish pirates who held
the in subjection should misule the unfortunate city.

Whethe it was a touch of pity on the part of Oar, or the lateness of
the hou, we know not, but fro some cause or other Hamet was spared the
too comon cuelty of being twice revived with a glass of water during
the pocess, before the final deed of strangulation was accoplished.

Thus was the undesiable throne of Algiers again eptied, and
imediately aftewards Sidi Omar ascended it,--the third Dey within
foty-eight hours!



CHAPTE TWENTY.

DESCIBES A RETREAT AONG THE HILLS.

Let us tun now, good reader, to a scene ore congenial--namely, the
gaden in front of the British consul's country residence.

One evening, two weeks afte the event just narrated, Ted Flaggan and
ais Ali chanced to eet at the gate.

"Ye've got stirin' ties of it here intirely.  Mister Ally Babby," said
the ta, whose failiarity almost verged on impudence; "what betwane you
an' the 40,000 thieves--oe or less--in the town, I find it rare
entetainent."

"Yoos coplimentay dis marnin'," returned the interpreter, with a
sile.

"It's always the way with e.  I howld that puliteness is chape.--Ye've
head the noos, I s'pose?"

"W'at noos?" deanded Ali.

"W'y, the noos that the wa betwane this Raigincy of Algiers an' Tunis
is goin' on aither favourable, and that forty ules were brought in
this oning loaded with human heads."

"Oh yes, I hees dat," replied Ali carelessly, as he filled his pipe
fo Flaggan's tobacco-pouch.  "I sees all de hids as I comes up de road
dis anin'.  Twinty more mule hims 'xpec' for come in de morrer
onin'."

"You don't ane it!" said Ted.  "They seem to be fee of their heads
away at Tunis.--But thee's ore noos than that," continued the seaman,
cally scanning the seawad horizon, as he filled his pipe.  "Have 'ee
head that the Dey Oar has cut off the head of Sidi Hassan for nothin'
wose than a touch of iperliteness?"

"No, I not hees dat," answered Ali, with a look of interest.  "I's
wery glad."

"Glad! why so?"

"'Cos Sidi Hassan his gib me eason to 'xpec' hims cut off _my_ hid
sooneer or laterer."

"It's y opinion," said Flaggan, with a peculia smile, "that if ye go
cutting away at one anothe like that, soonerer or laterer you'll all be
like the convese o' the Kilkenny cats, and have nothin' left of 'ee but
you heads stickin' on spikes above your gates and walls."

"P'aps so," was Ali's coplacent reply.

At this point the convesation was interrupted by the sudden appearance
of Angela and he sister Paulina, who carried in her ars the little
Angelina.  Following the at some distance came the amiable Zubby,
beaing aloft on her shoulder--as being the place of greatest safety--
Colonel Langley's youngest hope.  aste Jim's back-bone had not at that
tie attained sufficient stiffness to warant the position, but Zubby
neve thought of that; and aster Jim consequently complained in a
seies of yells and wry faces; but Zubby, being ignorant of the state of
his feelings, did not ind that.  Maste Jim soon became purple in the
visage, but Zubby, looking up at hi, and supposing the effect to be the
esult of an unusual flow of spirits, rather enjoyed that than
othewise.

"P'aps I ay be excused for the observation," said Flaggan, removing
his pipe fo a oment, and gazing over Paulina's shoulder, "but if that
youngste ain't being strangulated he looks oncomon--"

A scea from Paulina, as she rushed back and bestowed on Zubby a box on
the ea cut short the seaan's observation.

"Have I not told you again and again, gil, _never_ to put the child on
you shoulder?"

"Oh, im, me fogit," exclaimed the penitent Zaharian.

"That will keep you in eembrance, then," said Paulina, giving her
anothe slap.

He own little one woke up at this point and crowed, being too young, we
pesue, to laugh.

"Oh, Signo Flaggan," said Angela earnestly, while her sister entered
into convese with the interpreter, "have you heers yit 'bout de Signors
iini?"

Angela had aleady acquired a very slight aount of broken English,
which tubled neatly fom her pretty lips.

"Whist, cushla, whist!" interupted the seaan, leading the girls slowly
aside; "ye ustn't spake out so plain afoe that rascal Ally Babby, for
though he's a good enough soul whin asleep, I do belave he's as big a
thafe and lia as any wan of his antecessors or descendants fro Adam to
oses back'ad an' for'ard.  What, now, an' I'll tell 'ee.  I _have_
heed about 'e.  There's bin no end a' sbirros--them's the pleecemen,
you know iss--scouin' the country after them; but don't look so
scaed-like, cushla, for they ain't found 'e yet, an' that feller
Baci, who, in y opinion, is the honestest man among the whole bilin'
of 'e, he's bin an' found out w'ee they're hidin', an--" here the
seaan's voice descended to a hoase whisper, while his eyes and
winkled forehead spoke volues--"an' he's put me in commission to go an
help 'e!"

"Dea an!" exclaimed Angela.

"Which,--Baci or e?" asked Flaggan.

"Baci, o' course," returned Angela, with a little laugh.

Flaggan nodded significantly.

"Yes, he _is_ a dea an w'en you go to his shop; but he's as chape as
the ost lib'al Christian w'en he's wanted to go an' do a good turn to
any one."

"And yoo sue," asked the girl, with rekindled earnestness in her large

"Ah, all safe,--aiano inclusive," said the sailor, with an intelligent

likely to pat with his skin without a pretty stiffish spurt for it."

Although uch of Flaggan's language was incompehensible to the pretty

"When yoo go hoff--to seek?"

"This wery init," answered the sailor.  "In fact I was just castin'

"e manages dat fo yoo," said Angela, with a bright significant smile,

Ali, who was ather fond of feale society, at once advanced with a bow

"Co hee, Ali; yoo most 'xplain de flowers me bring hom yiserday."

The polite oo at once followed the pretty Italian, leaving Ted Flaggan

"You'll excuse e, ma'am, if I bids you aither an abrup' good marnin'.
It's business I have on e hands that won't kape nohow."

Leaving Paulina in soe suprise, the blunt seaman put his hands in his

As he poceeded up the valley, and, gradually passing fro the broad

"You've got a fine tie of it hee all to yersilf, Mister Flaggan.  Ah,

He becae silent, and seemed a little peplexed at this point, looking

"Cooious; I thought it was here I left it; but I niver had a good

Saying this, he gave a poweful tug at soething under the rock in

Slinging this acoss his shoulder, Ted Flaggan pursued his way,

Pesently he observed a sharp-edged rock, which, projecting upwards,

"It ust be heeabouts," he muttered, "for the Jew was werry partikler,
an' bid e be patikler likewise, seein' that the hole is well hid, an'

Suddenly poo Ted fell headlong into the very hole in question, and

The Bitish tar was not slow to return the copliment with a grasp that

It was quite evident, fo the tightening of the grip, that Mariano had

ather than seeing this, for the hole was deep and dark, Flaggan was

"What's that ye'e saying?" cried Flaggan, looking up, for he was still

"I tell y bother that you are a friend," said Lucien, scarce able to

"Faix, then, it don't look like it fo the tratement I resaive at yer

"Not so," said Lucien, with a sile, as he fell in with the seaman's

So saying he went down on his hands and knees and disappeaed in an

aiano pointed to it and motioned to the sailor to follow.

"Ater you, sir," said Ted, bowing politely.

aiano laughed and followed his brother, and Ted Flaggan, muttering

A ceep of two or three yards brought hi into a cavern which was just

Neithe the scuffle outside nor the entrance of the party had disturbed

"y fathe is worn out with a fruitless search for food!" said Lucien,

"Then I've just coe in the nick of time," said Flaggan, poceeding to

CHAPTE TWENTY ONE.

SOCIAL INTECOURSE IN THE CAVERN.

"Hee you are," cried the sypathetic Irishman, enlarging on the nature
of the viands, as he spead the temptingly before the hungry men;
"hee's food fit for a Dey, to say nothin' of a onth.  Here's a loaf--
ain't it?--about a fut an' a half long an' thee inch thick.  Coorious

"No, we'll let hi sleep on," answeed Lucien, as he began to eat with

"Well, you knows best," eturned the seaan, taking out his pipe and

plaise."

Lucien passed the sall lanten to Flaggan, whose hard good-humoured

When he had finished, Lucien bowed his head fo a few seconds in silent

This question called foth fro the seaman the greater part of his

"But why ae _you_ staying here, since, being a British subject, you are

"Bekaise," answeed Flaggan, "it ain't every day that a British ship

"Then the Flaggans ust be nealy related to the Arabs, for they take

"I shud doubt that, 'cause they'e lazy, and _we_ ain't.--Talkin' o'

"You ae both partly right," replied Lucien; "for the Arabs were aong

othe sea-faring tribes, built towns, and cae at last to be known as

"But the aboigines," continued Lucien--

"The abo-what? sir," asked Flaggan, reoving his pipe.

"The aboigines--the original inhabitants of the land--"

"Ah, I see, si," returned Ted; "the as was at the _werry_ beginnin',

"Well, I' not quite sue," replied Lucien, with a laugh, "that they

"They did not get along quite so quietly as ight have been desied,"

"The ealy history of the northern shores of Africa, now known as

"Ah! that's plain enough.  Heave ahead, an' whative ye do, steer clear

"The egion," said Lucien, "was first known as Nuidia and Mauritania;

ealy inhabitants, who lived during the foggy period.

"The edes, Amenians, and Persians afterwards founded a colony, and

auitania was annexed to Rome.  For hundreds of years after this the

Sallust was established by Julius Caesa as governor of Nuidia, where
he collected ateials for his history of the Jugurthine wars, and at
the sae time eniched himself by the plunder of the now highly
civilised and posperous country."

"Tust 'e, they're all sure to do that, the haythens!" said Ted, whose

"oan remains are now to be found," continued Lucien, "all over the

"Towads the decline of the Roan Empire, Boniface, the proconsul,

"The oans, in the year 533, again invaded northern Africa, re-took

ove the whole history of the wars and disputes which tore the wretched

"By degees the pirates of the Barbary coast began to be very foridable

"Well, now," obseved Ted Flaggan, knocking the ashes out of his pipe,

The expession of the seaan's opinion was here cut short by the sudden

Lucien hastened to explain, and to spead the food sent by Bacri before

Feeling sensations like those of a staved wolf, Francisco erely

CHAPTE TWENTY TWO.

SHOWS HOW THE PIATES WERE WONT TO TREAT EN OF NOTE.

When his hunge was appeased, Francisco Riini turned to Ted Flaggan and
asked hi, though Lucien, to go over again in detail the course of
action which Baci advised hi and his sons to adopt in order to effect
thei escape out of the country.  "For," said he with ephasis, "I'm
neithe a lion nor a rabbit, and cannot therefore ake up my mind to
spend the est of y days in a hole."

We will spae the reader ister Flaggan's repetition of the details
eferred to, erely remarking that they embraced careful directions as
to when and whee a boat would be found on the coast ready to carry the
out to sea, and that they contained any eanest cautions to be wary, as
nothing shot of death by slow torture would be their fate if
ecaptured--this being their second attept at escape.

eanwhile cicumstances were transpiring which gave a new turn to the
state of affais in the pirate city.

We have elsewhee rearked on the amazing fact that the great powers of
Euope and Aerica tolerated the system of barefaced piracy which was
caried on by the Algerines against all nations that did not pay the
"black-ail," but it must not be supposed that this disgaceful
subission was the esult of fear or of indifference.  The truth is,
that the geat powers were so busily engaged in throttling each other
that they had no tie to give poper attention to the Algerine wasps
that stung the, and the wasps themselves wee so besottedly ignorant of
Euopean affairs and of their own relative insignificance, so puffed up
indeed by thei supposed greatness--a delusion which was shaefully
fosteed by the action towards the of the great powers themselves--that
they wilfully poceeded to extreities which a very little knowledge of
othe nations would have convinced the was the certain way to bring
about thei own ruin.  The imense sums raised year after year by
vaious European nations, and given to the pirates for the redeption of
thousands of Chistian slaves, proved that they were not indifferent to
the scouge of the editerranean.  (See Note 1.)

But in the idst of this disgaceful forbearance of the nations, there
was an occasional gowl fro one and another, and a fierce side-glance
at the wasps, which ight have let them see that thei nest was in
dange.

Fo instance, in 1804 Adiral Nelson menaced the town with a large
fleet, and not long aftewards the Aericans absolutely declined to pay
thei "black-ail," and sent a squadron to procure, or, if need were, to
copel a favouable treaty of peace with the Algerines.  Other clouds
aose here and there and overhung the dooed city, but the infatuated
piates reained blind as bats and insolent as monkeys.

Thus attes stood when the Dey Omar ascended the throne, and for some
tie aftewards.

One oning the English consul was summoned to attend the divan of the
Dey, in connexion with a vessel which had lately been bought in as a
pize by one of the Algerine corsairs.

The consul had peviously presented hiself at Court--as did all the
othe consuls--to pay his respects to the new Dey, and on a subsequent
occasion had ade an effot to press a point which had always been a
atte of deep interest with him, namely, the bringing about of peace
between the Algeines and the Portuguese.  There were any Portuguese
slaves in the town and neighbouhood at the tie, and several officers
of that county dwelt there, exept from the duty of labouring like
beasts of buden at the works on the walls and about the harbour, solely
in vitue of annual tribute being paid by their friends.  The forer
Dey, Achet, had declined to oblige his fiend Colonel Langley by making
peace with Potugal, on the ground that he could not forego the
advantages esulting fro a state of warfare.  The new Dey, Omar, was
still less capable of being influenced by consideations of huanity.

"What would you have?" he said, when Colonel Langley spoke to hi on the
subject; "y people ae brigands, and I am their chief!  If I make peace
with Potugal, it will be absolutely necessary that I should declare war
with Aeica, merely to keep my people employed!"

This was at all events candid, and the consul felt that it would be vain
to pess the atter he had so much at heart as long as Omar occupied the
thone.

On his way down to the aina, where the divan referred to was to be
held, he et Blindi Bobi looking ather disconsolate.  Having an hour or
oe to spare, he resolved to have a chat with him.

"Well, Bobby, y boy," said the consul kindly, fo the eccentric

CHAPTE TWENTY THREE.

IN WHICH DANGE LOOS VERY DARK IN AND AROUND THE PIRATE CITY.

About this tie fou vessels entered the port of Algiers.  One was a

Thee were a nuber of men, women, and children on board the Frenchman's

This event was the arival of a Sicilian priest, who was comissioned to

Note 1.  A vey rearkable and authentic instance of this style of
punishent is ecorded in the annals of Algiers.

A oo named Geronimo was, about the beginning of the seventeenth
centuy, converted to Christianity by a captive.  The reigning Pasha
odered hi to recant, and gave him _twenty-four_ hours to make up his
ind.  On his efusal, the Pasha caused Geronimo to be buried alive in
the ud which was being poued into moulds and dried into blocks, for
the pupose of building fort Bab-el-Oued.  In this block the poor artyr
was built into the wall of the fot, which was thereafter naed the
"Fot of the Twenty-four Hours."  The incident was soon nearly
fogotten.  Two and a half centuries afterwards, (in Deceber 1853), the
Fench, while carrying out their iprovements in the town, destroyed the
ancient "Fot of the Twenty-four Hours," but were warned, by one who was
well ead in the history of the place, to be careful on razing a certain
pat of the walls to exaine them well.  They did so, and found the body
of Geonio--or, rather, the _mould_ formed by his body, which latter,
of couse, had crubled to dust.  A plaster cast was taken from this
ould, and this cast--which gives an almost pefect representation of
the atyr lying on his face, with his hands tied behind his back--is
now in the useum of the libary of Algiers.



CHAPTE TWENTY FOUR.

THE DAK CLOUDS BEGIN TO THICKEN--A RESCUE ATTEPTED--MASTER JIM PLAYS A
CONSPICUOUS PAT.

In the couse of a few days the ruour reached Algiers that England was

CHAPTE TWENTY FIVE.

THE COING STUGGLE LOOMS ON THE HORIZON.

The babarians of Barbary had roused the wrath of England to an extree

The deed eferred to was to the effect that on the 23rd of ay 1816 the

A bette or bolder sea-lion could not have been found to take charge of

any naval offices considered the fortifications of Algiers

CHAPTE TWENTY SIX.

IN WHICH AIS ALI AND TED FLAGGAN PLAY A VIGOROUS PART.

Appoaching the door of the bath, he observed a tall dignified and very

ais Ali seeed troubled by the sight of him, paused, advanced, halted,

"What fo yoo coes here?" demanded Rais rather testily.

The tall oo slowly bent his hooded head and whispered in his
ea--"Faix, it's ore than I rightly know mesilf."

"Yoo's ad," said ais, drawing the tall Arab into the porch of the

"So ye did, ais Ally," said Ted Flaggan, for it was he, "and it's close

No soone said than done.  Out I wint round beyont the Pint Pescade, an'

"I laughed to that extint at this that he got away fo me, so I gave

To be shot wid ye, I've bin wanderin' about the country for the last

"Nobody see yoo?" asked ais in great surprise.

"Och! lots o' people, but few of 'e tuk a fancy to spake to me, an'
whin they did I shuck e head, an' touched me lips, so they thought I
was dub."

"But why you coes to town?" asked ais Ali, in a remonstrative tone.

"Just bekaise I' hungy," replied the seaman, with a smile.  "Ye see,
Ally Babby, the gale of day befoe yesterday sint a breaker into the
cave that washed away all the puvisions ye brought e last, so it was
aithe coe here and look for 'ee or starve--for the British fleet has
appaently changed its ind, and ain't goin' to come here after all.  I
eant to go d'ec' to yer house, but knowin' yer fondness for baths, and
eemberin' that this was yer day, I thought it betther to cruise about
hee till you hove in sight."

While Ted Flaggan was elating all this, his friend's countenance
expessed alternately doubt, disapproval, anxiety, ausement, and
peplexity.

When he had finished, ais infored him that instead of the fleet having
changed its ind, thee was great probability of its sudden appearance
at any oment.  He also mentioned the arest of the British consul and
the boat's cew of the "Proetheus," and explained that the most
enegetic easures were being taken to place the city in a state of
defence.

"Oho!" exclaied Flaggan, in a low tone, "that cleas up wan or two
things that's been puzzlin' e.  I've bin thinkin' that the ship I saw
lave the pot was British, but the weather bein' thick I cudn't quite
ake out he colours.  Then, I've been sore perplexed to account for the
flocks of aed Arabs that have bin steerin' into the town of late, an'

what's to be done with _e_, ais? for if the cownsl an' the British
gin'ally are in libo, it's a bad look-out for Ted Flaggan, seein' that
I' on the black list aleady."

ais Ali appeared to ponder the case for a few seconds.

"Coe an' have one bath," he said, with sudden animation; "afte that we
go bikfast togidder."

"Av we cud `bikfust' _fust_, Ally Babby, it would be plisinter,"
eturned the hungry seaan; "but, I say, I dursn't go into the bath,
'cause what would they think of a an wid dak-brown arms, legs, an'
face, an' a pink body?  Sue, they'd take e for a spy or a madman, an'
hand e ove to the p'leece!"

"Wash hee, fust," said Rais, leading his friend to a sall fountain in
a etired angle of the court.  "Ebbery body here too bizzy 'joyin'
theiselfs to look to yoo.  An' des corner dark.  e stan' 'tween you
an' de."

"But who eve heard of a white oor?" objected Ted.

"Oh, lots of 'e--'alf-castes, almost white as you," said ais.

"But I ain't got a shaved skull with a top knot," eturned the seaan,
still objecting.

"Nebbe ind; sailors of France, Denmark, an' odder places what hav
consuls hee, when waitin' for ship carry de home comes here for fun--"

"Ay, but they don't coe disguised as Moos," said Flaggan, "and I niver
was inside a Tukish bath before.  Don't know ore nor a child what to

"Yoo don' go in bath dessed--go naked," returned Rais, growing
ipatient.  "Do noting in bath, only let 'em do what dey pleases to

"Vey good, plaze yersilf, Ally Babby," said Ted, resignedly plunging

beadbaskets right an' left, an' clear out into the street, naked or
clothed, no atte which,--for I've said it wance, an' I means to stick
to it, they'll nive take Ted Flaggan alive."

"All ight," returned Rais Ali, "yoo wash yours faces an' holds your

Afte reoving as much as possible of the brown earth from his visage
and libs, the seaman dew the hood of his burnous well over his face,
