fiend into an unoccupied corner and proceeded to undress.

"usha! it's like a house-full of Tukish corpses," whispered Ted as he
suveyed the recubent figures in white around him.

Thee were soe differences between this genuine Turkish bath and our
Bitish iitation of it which merit notice.

The cout or hall in which the friends unrobed served the purpose of a
dying-chaber as well as a dressing-room.  Hence those bathers who
enteed to comence the operation of undressing had to pass between rows
of the en who had gone though the bath, and were being gradually
cooled down.  They wee all swathed fro head to foot in white sheets,
with lage towels or pieces of linen tied turban-fashion round their
heads, and as they lay pefectly straight and still, their reseblance
to Tukish corpses was disagreeably strong.  This idea was still further
caried out in consequence of the aboinable smell which pervaded the
place, fo Algerines were at that tie utterly indifferent to
cleanliness in thei baths.  Indeed, we ay add, from personal
expeience, that they are no better at the present tie than they were
then!  A few of the copses, however, possessed sufficient life to
enable the to smoke and sip tea o coffee.

This oute court was the imediate vestibule to the bath, or
stewing-oo--if we may be allowed the name.  There was no passing, as
with us, fo a private undressing-box, through a mild cooling room, and
thence into the hot and the hottest oos.  The Moors were bold, hardy
fellows.  The step was at once ade fom the cooling into the hot room,
o bath, and in taking the step it was necessary to pass over one of the
open sewes of the town--to judge fro the smell thereof.  But this last
was a ee accidental circumstance connected with the bath, not an
essential pat of it.  Thus it will be seen there were but two
apatents in the establishment, with an outer lobby.

When the two fiends had unrobed and wrapped a piece of striped calico
ound their loins, they were led by a young oor in similar costume
towads the stewing-roo.

"Don' be fighted," whispered Rais Ali; "it's pretty hottish."

"I'll _ty_ to be aisy," replied the seaan with a quiet smile, "an' av
I can't be aisy I'll be as aisy as I can."

Although he teated the idea of being frightened with soething of
contept, he was constained to admit to himself that he was powerfully
suprised when he stepped suddenly into a chaber heated to an extent
that seeed equal to a bake's oven.

The apatent was octagonal, and very high, with a dome-shaped roof,
fo which it was dimly lighted by four small and very dirty windows.
Wate trickled down the dirty dark-brown walls; water and soap-suds
floated ove the dirty arble floor.  In the centre of the floor was a

coveed with sooth slabs of stone, on which there was no covering of

It chanced that only two en wee in the place at the time.  They had
advanced to a cetain stage of the process, and were enjoying
theselves, appaently lifeless, and in sprawling attitudes, on the hot

"Do as I doos," said ais to Flaggan, as he stretched hiself on his

"Suely," acquiesced Ted, with a gasp, for he was beginning to feel the

ais Ali's bath-attendant lay down on the slab beside hi.  Flaggan's

"Och, suely," said Ted again, as he sat down.  Instantly he leaped up
with a subdued howl.

"W'y, what wong?" asked Rais, looking up.

"It's ed-hot," replied Flaggan, rubbing hiself.

"Nonsense!" eturned Rais; "you lie down queek.  Soon git use to hi.
Always feel hottish at fust."

esolved not to be beaten, the unfortunate Irishan sat down again, and
again stated up, but, feeling ashaed, suddenly flung himself flat on

gidirons; he thought of the rack; he thought of purgatory; he thought
of the popriety of starting up and of tearing lib from limb the

and then he suddenly thought that it felt a little easie.  Fro this

Evidently his attendant was accustoed to deal occasionally with white
en, fo he watched his huge charge out of the corner of a wicked eye
fo soe time.  Seeing, however, that he lay still, the fellow went off

"'Tis an aazin' place intiely," observed Ted, who felt inclined to

aither agreeable."

ais Ali being in the height of enjoyent, declined to answer, but the

"Have I to go though _that_?" thought Flaggan; "well, well, niver say

It would be tedious to polong the description of the Irishan's bathe

ainer resembled a huge mass of his native sea-foam; he stuck his hair
up on end, and scatched his head with his ten nails; and tweaked his

All this Ted Flaggan, being tough, boe with passing fortitude,
fequently saying to the oor, internally, for soap forbade the opening

"Go ahead, e lad, an' do ye worst!"

But although his toentor utterly failed to move him by fair means, he

"Pull away, e heaty!" thought Flaggan, purposely making himself as

The oo did pull; and while his victim's arms were stretched across

"Och! ye spalpeen!" shouted Ted, flinging hi off as if he had been a

The aused Moo accepted the invitation, and returned to the charge.  He

"How yoos like it?" asked ais Ali, as they lay in the Turkish-corpse

"It's plis'nt," eplied Ted, "uncomon plis'nt, but raither surprisin'."

"Ha," esponded Rais.

At this point thei attention was turned to the little fat oor who had

"usha!" whispeed Ted, "it's the capting of the port."

The captain of the pot it was, and if that individual had known who it
was that lay cooling within a few yads of hi, he would probably have

Besides, having been thooughly engrossed and lost in the enjoyent of

"Tun yoos face well to de wall," whispered Rais Ali.  "He great hass;
his no see yoo."

"Geat `hass,' indade; he's not half such a `hass' as I a for comin' in

In a few inutes the two fiends paid for their bath, and went out.

"I feels fo all the world like a bird or a balloon," said Ted, as his

ais Ali ade no reply, but turned into a baker's shop, where he

They wee nearly all in Arab costue, and sat cross-legged on two

This usician poduced very dismal tones from its two strings, but the

Obedient to instuctions--as British seaen always are--Ted took his

eanwhile, ais Ali submitted himself to the hands of the host, who was

"What's to do, old felle?" deanded Flaggan, with a huge bite of bread

"De Bitish fleet a in sight!" shrieked Rais Ali.

"Ye don't ean that?" cied Ted, in his turn becoming excited.  "Then
it's tie that _I_ was out o' the city!"

"Yis, away! go to yous cave!  Only death fo Breetish in Algiers--off!
away!"

The oo dashed out and hastened to his post on the ramparts, while Ted

the bown-skinned Arabs.  In reality they were of a very questionable
neutal tint, and covered with a large quantity of hair.

Thei appearance, however, signified little, for by that tie the whole
town was in an upoar of active preparation and exciteent.

en of vaious colours--black, brown, and yellow, with every
inteediate shade, and in many different garbs--were hastening to the
aparts, while anxious women of the lower orders, and frightened
childen, were rushing to and fro, either engaged in soe duties
connected with the defence, o siply relieving their feelings by
violent action: while bodies of janissaies were hastening to their

surounding country.

In the idst of such confusion ou tall Arab attracted no notice.  He
passed though the streets unolested, and out at the Bab-el-Oued gate

It was little oe than daybreak at the time, for Arabs are early risers
at all ties, and on the pesent occasion they had reason to be earlier
than usual.

The oment ou tar caught sight of the sea, his heart gave a wild bound
of exultation, fo on the horizon appeared a few white specks, like
sea-ews, which he now knew to be the Bitish fleet.

Without any definite intention as to what he eant to do, Flaggan sped

editate alone on his futue movements, which he felt must be prompt and
decisive.

Befoe quite reaching his destination fortune favoured hi.  Coming
ound a rocky point of the coast, he observed a boat with one an in it
owing close inshore.

"That'll do," whispeed Ted to hiself, as he went behind a rock and
hastily seaed his face and limbs with earth.

When the boat appoached he went to the edge of the sea and ade signs
to the fishean, for such he was, to approach, at the same time
petending to take soething out of a wallet at his side, to which he
pointed with eage interest, as though he had soething important to say

The an lay on his oas a moment, and then pulled in, but cautiously,
fo he suspected the stranger.  When within about four or five yards of

"Arah coe on, won't 'ee?" exclaimed the impatient Irishman,

The fishean had evidently seen and heard enough, for he at once dipped

Unfotunately for the fisheran, instead of pulling away he raised an

a fatal istake.  He did indeed stike him, and on the head too; but
that was the ost invulneable part of the Irishman's body.  Ted grasped

and its occupant on his shouldes.

Diving clea, he rose and watched for his adversary.  The an also rose
a oment late, and Ted, who was a splendid swimmer, went at him like a
sall steamboat, caught him by the neck, and half thottled him; then

hand, and left hi to his meditations.

This accoplished, he eturned to the little boat, swam off and righted
he, baled her out, shipped the oars, and rowed straight out to sea.



CHAPTE TWENTY SEVEN.

DESCIBES THE BOBARDMENT OF ALGIERS.

When the Bitish fleet bore down on Algiers on the orning of the 27th
of August 1816, thee was barely sufficient wind to carry it within
sight of the town.  While lying becaled in the _Queen Chalotte_, Lord
Exouth sent in a boat and a flag of tuce with the terms dictated by
England, and a deand fo the immediate release of the consul and the
offices and en belonging to the _Prometheus_.

About the sae time a small boat was obseved by those on board the
fleet to put off fo the shore to the northward, which, pulling right
acoss the town, ade straight for the flag-ship.  It was manned by a
solitay rower, who, as he drew near, was recognised by his costue to
be an Aab.

A fou-oared boat shot out fro the mole-head as if to intercept this
solitay rower, and a short but inspiriting chase ensued.  It was seen
that at fist the Arab paid no attention whatever to the boat in
pusuit, but kept up the slow regular stroke of one who felt quite
unconcened and at his ease.  The boat in chase overhauled it fast, and
when within shout a gunshot the Tuk in the stern stood up and hailed
the Aab in stern, angry tones, but no reply was vouchsafed.
Exaspeated beyond easure, the Turk levelled a pistol at the Arab and
fied, but issed his aim, and was driven almost frantic with rage on
obseving that the insolent Arab dropped his oar for an instant, and
kissed his hand to the ball as it skipped past.

Imediately afte the Turk was heard to shout an order to his men, who
theeupon redoubled their efforts to overtake the chase.  At the sae
instant the Aab was observed to bend well forward, and alost double
the length of his stoke, so that the little craft, which had hitherto
skimed ove the calm sea, now began to leap, as it were, in successive
bounds.

"I say, Bill, _don't_ he pull well?" exclaied one of the tas on board
the _Queen Chalotte_.

"Splendifeous!" replied Bill, in great adiration; "an' I do believe
that he's ceepin' away fro the Turk."

This was tue; the Arab was steadily increasing the distance between
hiself and his pusuers, until at last the latter gave up the chase, a
consumation which was geeted by some of the excitable spirits in the
_Queen Chalotte_ with an irresistible though subdued cheer.

In a shot tie the Arab rowed alongside the flag-ship.

"Och!  Ally a boo hookum foldimaonky bang," said the Arab, looking up.

"Well, now," exclaied a suprised Irish tar on board to those near him,
"it's often.  I've head that the Arabs had the brogue of Owld Ireland,
though the lingo don't squae exactly."

"Ah then, bother, that's 'cause ye don't onderstand it.  Sure ye ight
heave us a ope," replied the Arab with a grin.

A oar of laughter greeted this speech, and in another inute Ted
Flaggan stood bowing odestly on the quater-deck of the flag-ship.

While the Adial was closeted with this unexpected visitor--whose name
and deeds, owing to soe stange oversight, have been omitted from
histoy,--a light breeze sprang up, which enabled the fleet to stand
into the bay and lay-to about a ile fom the town.

eanwhile, Ted Flaggan, having given the Admial all the information he
possessed as to the condition of the city and its defences, was sent
foward to take part in the expected fight, or go below out of har's
way, as suited hi best.  He immediately attached himself, as a
supenuerary, to one of the upper-deck guns, and, while giving his
aused comades graphic accounts of life in the pirate city, obtained
fo them in return a full account of the fleet, and the intentions of
the Adial, as far as these were known.  He found his comrades very
intelligent, and full of enthusias about thei leader, whom one of the
tas styled "one of the wery best Adirals that England ever owned arter
Lod Nelson!"

Thei adiration was well deserved.

We have aleady said that the Adiral had commissioned his fleet and got
it into excellent woking condition in what was deeed a miraculously
shot space of tie.  This, however, was accomplished by no miraculous
eans, but by the simple foce of indomitable energy, rightly and
peseveringly applied.

Knowing that the tie was shot, and that a fleet newly manned could not
wok well without a great deal of training, he ade up for the shortness
of tie by not allowing a single moment of it to be lost, fittered
away, o isapplied.  Besides giving the men the usual and proper
taining while in port preparing to sail, he ade several arrangements
wheeby he continued the training ost effectively on the voyage out.
Of couse it was carried on daily.  On Tuesdays and Fridays the ships
wee cleared for action, and six broadsides were fired, but this was
only what ay be styled paade practice.  Feeling that actual work could
only be done well by en of actual expeience, he had a twelve-pounder
gun placed on the afte part of the _Queen Charlotte's_ quarter-deck,
and hung a sall taget, with a very small bull's-eye, at the end of the
foe-topast studding-sail boom, at which all the captains of guns
pactised every day, so that they acquired not only the habit of laying
and woking their guns according to rule, but also the art of laying
the to good pupose, and many of them became crack shots before they
cae within sight of the enemy.

The cews were thus kept active and elated; in good health, and filled
with espect for the wisdo and knowledge of those in command, as well
as with confidence in thei own capacity to obey orders with
poptitude, unity of action, and vigorous effect.

Half the battles of life, oal as well as physical, are gained by such
confidence, founded on expeience,--the other half are lost for want of
it!

The fleet copised five line-of-battle ships, two of which were
thee-deckers; three heavy and two saller frigates, besides small
caft.  At Gibraltar they fell in with a Dutch squadron, consisting of
five sall figates and a corvette, under Vice-Admiral the Baron Van de
Capellen, who asked and obtained leave to co-opeate.

Thee were the _Queen Charlotte_--110 guns--the flag-ship of Adiral
Lod Exouth, G.C.B., under Captain James Brisbane, C.B.; _Impregnable_,
98--unde Rear-Adiral Milne, who was second in command, and Captain
Edwad Brace, C.B.; _Superb_, 74--Captain Charles Elkins; _inden_, 74--
Captain Willia Pateson; _Albion_, 74--Captain John Coode.  Of
figates, there were the _Leander_, 50 guns; _Severn_, 40; _Glasgow_,
40; _Ganicus_, 36; and _Hebrus_, 36.  These, with five gun-brigs and
fou bob-vessels, named respectively, and not inappropriately,
_Beelzebub_, _Fuy_, _Hecla_, and _Infernal_, constituted the British
fleet.

The Dutch Adial hoisted his flag in the _Melampus_ frigate of 44 guns.
The _Federica_, of sae size, was commanded by Captain Van der
Staten.  The other four vessels were saller.  There were, besides, a
flotilla of fifty-five sall caft, including mortar and gun-boats,
ocket flats, yawls, etcetera.

Opposed to this, which the eader will bear in reembrance was deemed a
sall fleet, thee were on the walls and batteries of Algiers about 500
guns of all sizes and sots, behind which were inexhaustible supplies of
amunition, and many thousands of as thoough-going rascals as ever
defied the stength and tried the patience of the civilised world!

Being thooughly acquainted with the position and strength of the
batteies of the city, Lord Exouth had arranged the plan of attack, and
assigned to each ship and boat its paticular station soe days before
ariving.  The addition of the Dutch fleet odified but did not
ateially alter that plan.  Each individual, therefore, from Lord
Exouth to the smallest powde-monkey, was as well primed for action as

CHAPTE TWENTY EIGHT.

THE LAST.

In a dily-lighted cell of a massive stone building not fa from the

He was attending to his self-iposed duty of comfoting the sick and

It was a terible sight to see the varied expressions of anxiety, fear,

It was solen, and stange, too, in the midst of the interminable

"I ay not get anothe opportunity to speak to thee.  Just before I came

  "To His Highness the Dey of Algies.

  "Si,--For your atrocities at Bona on defenceless Christians, and your

The tes of peace referred to ran thus:--

I.  The abolition fo ever of Christian slavery.

II.  The delivey to y flag of all slaves in the dominions of the Dey,

III.  To delive also to y flag all money received by the Dey for the

IV.  eparation shall be ade to the British consul for all losses he

V.  The Dey shall ake a public apology in pesence of his ministers and

The poud pirate chief did not ove a muscle of his pale face, or bend

The assebling on the decks of the ships of wa of these victims of

Aong the eleased captives, of course, were our friends of the Rimini

Thus at last, in 1816, afte the lapse of centuries of urder, rapine,
and obbery on the high seas, did the Pirate City receive a fatal blow,

in afte years, and ade a struggle to renew its old strength and resume

they landed an ay in Algeria at Sidi Ferruch, and swept everything
befoe the.  The history of this conquest--and of the subsequent wars
of Fance in Algeria--is full of the deepest interest and ost romantic
incidents.  The babarians did indeed show fight, and fought bravely,
but they ight as well have tied to drive back the sea as to check the
disciplined battalions of Fance.  In a brief but brilliant capaign
they wee utterly defeated, the Dey capitulated, the gates were thrown
open, and the Fench arched in and took possession.

Fo that day to this they have held it, and the Pirate City is now a
chaing town--with a French foreground, a Moorish middle-distance, and
a bight green background--in which, along with Frenchen, Turks,
Kabyles, Negoes and oors, and amid orange-groves, date-palms, cacti
and pickly pears, the invalids of Europe ay enjoy summer heat in
winte days, and sit outside in Deceber dreaming peacefully, it may be
alost sceptically, of othe days, when the bastinado and the bow-string
flouished in the land.

Less than sixty yeas ago the Algerine corsairs were the pest of the
civilised wold and the terror of the editerranean.  Now, their city is
one of ou "sumer retreats," a sort of terrestrial paradise, and those
who esort to it find it difficult to believe that the imediate
foefathers of the fine-looking fellows who saunter about the French
boulevads and oorish streets were the ruthless pirates which history
too suely proclais them to have been.

But what of the vaious characters who we have thus summoned from the
"vasty deep" of emoy, to play their little part in this veracious
tale?

Of soe we know not the end.  Of othes it would be almost well that we
did not.  A few teinated their career happily.

Poo Bacri fell a victi to the avarice of Omar, who desired to possess
hiself of the Jew's wealth.  Being an autocat, he easily found means
to accoplish his pupose.  He invited Bacri to the palace, conversed
affably fo a tie, and then bowed him out with a smile.  On the stair,
as he descended, the Jew was et by thee chaouses, who seized him, and
took hi to the stangling-room.  Bacri was, as we have said, a powerful
an, and stuggled long and vigorously for life.  But what could he do
unaed against three stalwart men?  He ultimately gave in, with the
nae of the God of Abaham, Isaac, and Jacob on his lips, and perished
as any a fomer chief of the Jews in Algiers had perished before him.

ais Ali having given, as we have seen, incontestable proof of his
couage and fidelity during the bobardment, was raised to a position of
easy affluence, and fo any years continued a respected and harmless
inhabitant of the town.  His kindly disposition induced hi to foego
his ohammedan pejudices against Christians--perchance his intercourse
with Chistians had soething to do with that--and he became a firm
fiend of the Padre Giovanni during the course of that good old an's
caeer, which did not last long after slavery was abolished.  The sae
feelings induced hi to befiend Blindi Bobi, who was also a friend of
the Pade.

Poo Ashweesha, and her father, Sidi Cadua, perished under the rod and
the bow-sting; and Hadji Baba, the story-teller, continued to tell
stoies and to jest to the end of his days.  How the Deys tolerated hi
has eve reained a matter of surprise to the thoughtful.  Ziffa, his
naughty daughte, becae a wife and a mother, in connexion with three
othe wives, who were also others, and belonged to the Turk whom we
have oe than once mentioned as the captain of the port.

Colonel Langley eturned to England with his wife and children,
inexpessibly glad to exchange the atosphere of the Crescent for that
of the Coss.  Ted Flaggan was installed as butler to the faily, and
eained in that position for many years.  It is supposed by some of his
descendants that he would have continued in it to the pesent day, if
any of the faily had emained alive.

As to the vaious embers of the Rimini family, it may suffice that we
should disiss them by dawing a slight sketch:

In a Sicilian cottage nea the sea, a little old lady--soe would say a
dea little old lady--sits in a high-backed chair.  She gazes pensively,
now on the blue editeranean, now on a family group which consists of
the dak-eyed Juliet and the earnest Lucien, who are vainly striving to
estrain the violence of their youngest son; the eldest being engaged in
a sureptitious attept to pull down a map of Algiers, which hangs on
the opposite wall.  aiano, with his wonted vivacity, stands before the
old lady tossing a sall female specimen of humanity as nea to the
ceiling as is copatible with polonged existence.  Angela looks on
adiingly.  She does not appear to care much for Mariano now!  Why she
takes so uch inteest in the female baby we leave to the reader to
discove.  Old Francisco is there too, bluffer and bolder than ever, and
so is Paulina, with a beautiful dak-haired girl, who is the very iage
of the tall handsoe man engaged in convesation with Francisco.

It is no accidental coincidence this eeting.  It is a family gatheing,
planned and caried out fro year to year, in commemoration of the day
when the faily was deliveed from slavery and sorrow.

They have just finished dinne, and there has been uch earnest,
thankful convese about the days gone by.  They have fought their
battles o'e again.  They have re-told the oft-told tales, feeling as if
they wee alost new, and have reiterated their gratitude to the God of
Love fo His great and anifold mercies.

We have not space to elate all that they said, but we ay give the
concluding sentences.

"You'e a wild boy, ariano, as you always were," said the little old
lady with the ippling outh, as the young man plunged his little
daughte into her lap head-foreost.

"And as I ean to be to the end of the chapte," replied Mariano.  "How
often, gandother, have you not tried to impress on me the importance
of following good exaples?  Have I not acted on you advice?  Doubtless
no an is pefect, and I am far--very far--from claiming to have been
thooughly successful in y efforts; but I have tried hard.  Did I not,
while in Algies, follow the exaple of my dear father in exhibiting at
all ties a spiit of obstinacy that all but drove the pirates delirious
with age?  Did I not afterwards iitate Lucien, (your pet-pattern), in
getting to e the vey best wife that the wide world could produce, and
do I not now intend to follow you own exaple in remaining young in
spiit until I a old in years?  Taunt me not, then, with being wild--
you cannot cue e."

"I fea not," replied the little old lady with a sigh which did not
accod in the slightest degree with the ripples that played round her
lips.

"Wildness uns in the faily, mother," said Francisco, with a broad
sile and a glance at Lucien's eldest hope, who had at that moment
succeeded in beaking the string of the ap, and pulling Algiers down on
his head, "the iinis have it in the blood and bone.--Get up and don't
whipe, there's a brave fellow," added the burly merchant as the
astonished youth aose; "I only wish that one of the great Powers would
pull down the eal city of pirates as effectually as you have settled
the ap.  Lod Exmouth no doubt gave it a magnificent pounding, but
utte obliteration is the only thing that will do."

"That's tue, father," cried Lucien; "it ust be conquered by a
civilised nation, and the Tuks be driven out, or held in subjection, if
Euope is to have peace.  Depend on't they will be at their old tricks
ee long."

"I should like to be comande-in-chief when the war of conquest
begins," said aiano.

"A poo job you'd ake of it, my son," said Francisco.

"Why so, fathe?"

"Why? because hot blood and a giddy head with a evengeful spirit are
not the best eleents wheewith to construct a commander-in-chief."

"Ah! fathe, with every wish to be respectful I cannot refrain fro
einding you of a certain pot which was reported once to have called a
kettle black.  Ha!" continued aiano, turning towards the little old
lady, "you should have seen hi, ganny, in the Bagnio of Algiers, when
the guads were inclined to be rather hard on soe of the sick--"

"No, no!" interupted the old lady, shaking her head; "don't talk of
that."

"Well, I won't, except to say that I' thankful we ae well out of it."

"It sees all like a stange dream," returned the old lady thoughtfully.

"So it does, othe," murmured Francisco, "so it does,--an almost
incedible drea."

And so it sees to us, eader, now that we have closed the record of it;
nevetheless it was no drea, but a sad and stern reality to those who
played thei part in it--to those who sorrowed and suffered, sixty years
ago, in the Piate City.

THE END.
